      The Iron Age
        The last age is of hard iron, and deadly Night bare Nemesis to afflict mortal men. de duro est ultima ferro, Τίκτε gave birth δὲ postpositional but καὶ and Νέμεσιν accusative Nemesis, πῆμα misery θνητοῖσι mortal βροτοῖσι dative mortal men, human beings, Νὺξ f Nox; night ὀλοή destructive, deadly· And last She bore obstinate-souled Strife. καὶ and Ἔριν strife τέκε gave birth καρτερόθυμον strong/obstinate/violent soul. Immediately every impiety bursts into the age of worse vein; protinus inrupit venae peioris in aevum omne nefas: Modesty and truth and faith fled; fugere perfect fled pudor modesty verumque and truth fidesque and faith, loyalty, honesty, trust; in whose place came fraud, crime, treachery, violence, and the wicked love of possessing things. in into quorum genitive whose subiere perfect came locum place fraudesque dolusque
        insidiaeque et vis et amor sceleratus habendi.         Moreover, abhorred Strife bare painful Toil and Forgetfulness and Famine and tearful Sorrows, Αὐτὰρ but, however, moreover Ἔρις strife στυγερὴ τέκε gave birth μὲν Πόνον ἀλγινόεντα
        Λήθην τε and Λιμόν famine, hunger, starvation τε καὶ and Ἄλγεα pain δακρυόεντα tearful         The sailor spread his sails to winds not yet known, and ship keels that long had stood on lofty mountains pierced uncharted waves. vela dabant ventis nec adhuc bene noverat illos
        navita, quaeque prius steterant in montibus altis,
        fluctibus ignotis insultavere carinae,         And the cautious surveyors marked with long boundaries the ground, which before was as free as the light of the sun or as the air. communemque prius ceu lumina solis et auras
        cautus humum longo signavit limite mensor.         And the rich ground was asked to provide more than merely crops and nourishment, and men went into the bowels of the earth to dig up the riches (the incentives of evil) which had been hidden in the infernal deep caves. nec tantum segetes alimentaque debita dives
        poscebatur humus, sed itum est in viscera terrae,
        quasque recondiderat Stygiisque admoverat umbris,
        effodiuntur opes, inritamenta malorum.         And now hurtful iron and gold (more hurtful than iron) had come forth; and War came forth, which uses both, and shakes together rattling arms with bloody hand. iamque nocens ferrum ferroque nocentius aurum
        prodierat, prodit bellum, quod pugnat utroque,
        sanguineaque manu crepitantia concutit arma.         Fightings also, Battles, Murders, Manslaughters, Quarrels, Lying Words, Disputes, Lawlessness and Ruin, all of one nature, life is kept up by plunder and pillage. Ὑσμίνας battle, conflict, combat τε and Μάχας τε and Φόνους τ᾽ and Ἀνδροκτασίας τε and
        Νείκεά τε and ψευδέας τε and Λόγους Ἀμφιλλογίας τε and
        Δυσνομίην τ᾽ and Ἀάτην τε and, συνήθεας ἀλλήλῃσιν,         vivitur ex rapto:         And Oath, who most troubles men upon earth when anyone wilfully swears falsely. Ὅρκον the object by which one swears, the witness of an oath; an oath θ᾽ and, ὃς this δὴ adding emphasis, truly πλεῖστον most ἐπιχθονίους earthly, dwelling on Earth ἀνθρώπους accusative plural humanity, all of mankind
        πημαίνει misery, ὅτε when κέν would τις someone ἑκὼν willing ἐπίορκον false swearing, perjury ὀμόσσῃ same, common, joint.         The guest was not protected from his host, nor the father-in-law from his own son-in-law; even brothers seldom could abide in peace. non hospes ab hospite tutus,
        non socer a genero, fratrum quoque gratia rara est;
         The husband threatened to destroy his wife, and she her husband; horrid step-mothers mixed lurid poisons; eager sons inquired their fathers' age before their appointed day; inminet exitio vir coniugis, illa mariti,
        lurida terribiles miscent aconita novercae,
        filius ante before diem patrios inquirit in annos:         Piety lies vanquished; and the virgin Astraea is the last of the celestials to leave the blood-soaked earth. victa iacet pietas, et virgo caede madentis
        ultima caelestum terras Astraea reliquit.
         And so the people of the world lived in an Iron Age for five hundred years. Et sic mundus esset apud aetas ferro quingenti annis.

      Gigantomachy

      Gigantomachy
        And lest the higher aether should long remain less troubled than the earth, it is recorded that the Giants aspired to the celestial kingdom, and built up mountains heaped together to the lofty stars. Neve foret terris securior arduus aether,
        adfectasse ferunt regnum caeleste gigantas
        altaque congestos struxisse ad sidera montis.
        Ossa
        a mountain in Thessaly, Greece, southeast of Olympus
        Pelion
        a mountain in southeastern Thessaly, Greece, southeast of Ossa

        The Giants had piled Pelion onto Ossa to attack the Gods in Olympus

        Then the all-powerful Father broke through Olympus with his hurled thunderbolt, and threw Mount Pelion down from Ossa below it. tum pater omnipotens misso perfregit Olympum
        fulmine et excussit subiecto Pelion Ossae.         When their dead bodies lay overwhelmed by their own mass, they tell that the earth, over sprinkled with much blood of her children, grew moist, and animated the warm gore: lest no monuments of the savage stock should remain, converted it into the form of men. obruta mole sua cum corpora dira iacerent,
        perfusam multo natorum sanguine Terram
        immaduisse ferunt calidumque animasse cruorem
        et, ne nulla suae stirpis monimenta manerent,
        in faciem vertisse hominum;         But also that progeny was a scorner of the Gods above, and most greedy of cruel slaughter, and violent. You would know they were born from blood. sed et illa propago
        contemptrix superum saevaeque avidissima caedis
        et violenta fuit: scires e sanguine natos.         When Father Saturn saw this from His high throne, He groans. Quae pater ut summa vidit Saturnius arce,
        ingemit.
      The DelugeAt first Saturn would call upon Lua Saturni to bring down destruction upon the earth, but He feared, once set free on Her own, that She would overwhelm even the whole universe. A different punishment seems good, to destroy the mortal race under the waters, and to send down rains from all the sky. Luam Saturni invocaret primo deducere exitium
      terrae qui autem timore perterritus, totum semel
      set liberum sui est, eam universum disperdat.
      poena placet diversa, genus mortale sub undis
      perdere et ex omni nimbos demittere caelo.       Immediately He closes in Aeolian caves the Northwind and every other wind that might dispel the gathering clouds and sends forth the Southwind. Protinus Aeoliis Aquilonem claudit in antris
      et quaecumque fugant inductas flamina nubes
      emittitque Notum.       Auster the south wind flies forth on moist wings, concealing in the mist his awful face;
      madidis Notus evolat alis,
      terribilem picea tectus caligine vultum;       the drenching rain descends from his beard and waves flow from his white hair; barba gravis nimbis, canis fluit unda capillis; mists sit upon his forehead, and his feathers and folds drop dew. fronte sedent nebulae, rorant pennaeque sinusque. And when he pressed the hanging clouds with his broad hand, a crash is made: hence heavy showers are poured from the sky. utque manu lata pendentia nubila pressit,
      fit fragor: hinc densi funduntur ab aethere nimbi;       Iris Rainbow, the messanger of Ops, arrayed in various colors, collects waters and brings supplies to the clouds. nuntia Opis varios induta colores
      concipit Iris aquas alimentaque nubibus adfert.       The crops are laid low and the hopes of the farmers lie lamenting, and the ineffectual labor of the long year perishes. sternuntur segetes et deplorata coloni
      vota iacent, longique perit labor inritus anni.       Nor was the anger of Saturn content with His own sky, His own azure brother helps him with aiding waters. Nec caelo contenta suo est Saturni ira, sed illum
      caeruleus frater iuvat auxiliaribus undis.       Oceanus calls together the rivers, who when they entered the roof of their king, He says “A long appeal is needless; bring forth in rage of powers, open your fountains, rush over obstacles, let every stream pour forth in boundless floods,” He had commanded. convocat hic amnes: qui postquam tecta tyranni
      intravere sui, 'non est hortamine longo
      nunc' ait 'utendum; vires effundite vestras:
      sic opus est! aperite domos ac mole remota
      fluminibus vestris totas inmittite habenas!'
      iusserat;       The servants return and let loose mouths to their fountains and roll with unbridled course into the sea. hi redeunt ac fontibus ora relaxant
      et defrenato volvuntur in aequora cursu.       And He strikes the earth with his trident, which trembles and lay open its bosom of water with the shock. Ipse tridente suo terram percussit, at illa
      intremuit motuque vias patefecit aquarum.       The Rivers spreading rush through the open plains and snatch away groves together with sown wheat, and flocks, and men, and dwellings, and sanctuaries with their sacred things. exspatiata ruunt per apertos flumina campos
      cumque satis arbusta simul pecudesque virosque
      tectaque cumque suis rapiunt penetralia sacris.       If any house remained and undemolished was able to resist so great an evil, nevertheless the wave, being higher, covers its top and its tower pressed down totter under the eddy. si qua domus mansit potuitque resistere tanto
      indeiecta malo, culmen tamen altior huius
      unda tegit, pressaeque latent sub gurgite turres.       And now the sea and land had no distinction; all things were a sea. iamque mare et tellus nullum discrimen habebant:
      omnia pontus erat,       Shores also were wanting to the sea. derant quoque litora ponto. One man occupies a hill; another sits in a hollow boat, and there draws his oars where he had always ploughed. Occupat hic collem, cumba sedet alter adunca
      et ducit remos illic, ubi nuper arabat:       That man sails above corn crops or the roofs of a sunken villa; this catches a fish on the top of an elm. ille supra segetes aut mersae culmina villae
      navigat, hic summa piscem deprendit in ulmo.       The anchor is fixed in a green meadow, if chance has directed it, figitur in viridi, si fors tulit, ancora prato, or curved keels rubs against vineyards placed under them; aut subiecta terunt curvae vineta carinae; and where just now slender she-goats cropped the grass, there now misshapen sea-calves place their bodies. et, modo qua graciles gramen carpsere capellae,
      nunc ibi deformes ponunt sua corpora phocae.       The Nereids admire groves and cities and houses under the water. mirantur sub aqua lucos urbesque domosque
      Nereides, silvasque tenent delphines et altis       And dolphins possess woods, course over the high boughs, and beat the shaken oaks. silvasque tenent delphines et altis
      incursant ramis agitataque robora pulsant.       The wolf swims among the sheep, nat lupus inter oves, the wave carries tawny lions, fulvos vehit unda leones, the wave carries tigers. unda vehit tigres; Nor do his powers of lightning avail the boar nor his swift legs the stag carried away. nec vires fulminis apro,
      crura nec ablato prosunt velocia cervo,       And having sought for land a long time, where it might be granted to settle, the wandering bird with wearied wings, falls down into the sea. quaesitisque diu terris, ubi sistere possit,
      in mare lassatis volucris vaga decidit alis.       The unbounded license of the ocean had overwhelmed the hillocks and new waves were beating the mountain tops. obruerat tumulos inmensa licentia ponti,
      pulsabantque novi montana cacumina fluctus.       The greatest part is hurried away by the wave; the long fastings subdue by scanty food those whom the water has spared. maxima pars unda rapitur; quibus unda pepercit,
      illos longa domant inopi ieiunia victu.       Phocis, a rich land, while it was land, separates the Aonians from the Aetolian fields, but in that time it was a part of the sea and a broad plain of sudden waters. Separat Aonios Oetaeis Phocis ab arvis,
      terra ferax, dum terra fuit, sed tempore in illo
      pars maris et latus subitarum campus aquarum.       There a lofty mountain, Parnassus by name, seeks the stars with two peaks, and surmounts the clouds with its top. mons ibi verticibus petit arduus astra duobus,
      nomine Parnasos, superantque cacumina nubes.       When Deucalion, carried on a small raft with the partner of his bed, stuck here (for the sea had covered the rest), they adore the Corycian nymphs and the Deities of the mountain, and the prophetic Themis, who then held the oracle. hic ubi Deucalion (nam cetera texerat aequor)
      cum consorte tori parva rate raft vectus adhaesit,
      Corycidas nymphas et numina montis adorant
      fatidicamque Themin, quae tunc oracla tenebat:       There was not any man better than he, nor more loving of equity; or any woman more fearing of the Gods than she. non illo melior quisquam nec amantior aequi
      vir fuit aut illa metuentior ulla deorum.       When Saturn sees that the world lies stagnant with liquid pools and that one man alone survives from so many thousands just now; and sees that one woman survives of so many thousands just now; innocent, both worshippers of the Deity; He dispersed the clouds, and the showers having been removed by the Northwind, He showed both the lands to the sky, and the aether to the lands. Saturnus ut liquidis stagnare paludibus orbem
      et superesse virum de tot modo milibus unum,
      et superesse vidit de tot modo milibus unam,
      innocuos ambo, cultores numinis ambo,
      nubila disiecit nimbisque aquilone remotis
      et caelo terras ostendit et aethera terris.       Nor does the anger of the sea remain, and the Ruler of the ocean soothes the waters, his three-pointed weapon being laid aside, and he calls caerulean Triton standing up above the deep, and covered as to his shoulders with native purple; and He orders Him to breathe into his sounding shell, and already to recall the waves and the floods at a given signal. nec maris ira manet, positoque tricuspide telo
      mulcet aquas rector pelagi supraque profundum
      exstantem atque umeros innato murice tectum
      caeruleum Tritona vocat conchaeque sonanti
      inspirare iubet fluctusque et flumina signo
      iam revocare dato:       The hollow-wreathed bugle-horn, which increases from the lowest bend into a broad top, is taken by Him, cava bucina sumitur illi,
      tortilis in latum quae turbine crescit ab imo,       the bugle, which, when it has collected air in the midst of the sea, fills with its voice the shores lying under either sun. bucina, quae medio concepit ubi aera ponto,
      litora voce replet sub utroque iacentia Phoebo;       Then also when it touched the lips of the God, dropping dew from his moist beard, and being blown, sounded the commanded retreat of the waters, it was heard by all the waters of the earth and of the sea, tum quoque, ut ora dei madida rorantia barba
      contigit et cecinit iussos inflata receptus,
      omnibus audita est telluris et aequoris undis,       and by whatever waters it was heard, it restrained all of them. et quibus est undis audita, coercuit omnes. Already the sea has a shore, their channel contains the full rivers, iam mare litus habet, plenos capit alveus amnes, the floods sink down and the hills are seen to come forth, flumina subsidunt collesque exire videntur; the ground rises, grounds increase by the waters decreasing, surgit humus, crescunt sola decrescentibus undis, and after a long day the woods show their tops naked and hold slime left on their foliage. postque diem longam nudata cacumina silvae
      ostendunt limumque tenent in fronde relictum
      Repopulating the World
        The globe was restored; which when Deucalion saw empty, and that the desolated lands kept deep silence, he addresses Pyrrha thus, with gushing tears: Redditus orbis erat; quem postquam vidit inanem
        et desolatas agere alta silentia terras,
        Deucalion lacrimis ita Pyrrham adfatur obortis:         “O my sister! O spouse! O woman, sole surviving, whom a common race and kindred origin, and then the marriage bed, hath joined to me; and now themselves join. We two are the crowd of all the lands, whatever the setting and the rising look upon. The sea has taken possession of the rest. 'o soror, o coniunx, o femina sola superstes,
        quam commune mihi genus et patruelis origo,
        deinde torus iunxit, nunc ipsa pericula iungunt,
        terrarum, quascumque vident occasus et ortus,
        nos duo turba sumus; possedit cetera pontus.         “now also confidence in our life is not as yet sufficiently certain: even now the clouds terrify my mind. haec quoque adhuc vitae non est fiducia nostrae
        certa satis; terrent etiamnum nubila mentem.         “O you to be pitied, what feeling would arise now to you if you had been snatched from the fates without me? quis tibi, si sine me fatis erepta fuisses,
        nunc animus, miseranda, foret?         “In what manner could you bear your fear alone? Who consoling could you bear your sorrows? quo sola timorem
        ferre modo posses? quo consolante doleres!         “For I, (believe me), if only the sea had you, would follow you, O spouse, and the sea should have me likewise. namque ego (crede mihi), si te quoque pontus haberet,
        te sequerer, coniunx, et me quoque pontus haberet.         “O I wish that I were able to repair the nations by my father’s arts, and to infuse souls into the molded earth; now the mortal race rests in us two; thus it hath seemed to the gods above, and we remain patterns of human beings.” o utinam possim populos reparare paternis
        artibus atque animas formatae infundere terrae!
        nunc genus in nobis restat mortale duobus.
        sic visum superis: hominumque exempla manemus.'         He had spoken, and they wept. It seemed good to pray to the celestial divinity; and to seek aid by sacred lots. dixerat, et flebant: placuit caeleste precari
        numen et auxilium per sacras quaerere sortes.         There is no delay: together they go to the waters of the Cephisus as not yet clear so already cutting known channels. nulla mora est: adeunt pariter Cephesidas undas,
        ut nondum liquidas, sic iam vada nota secantes.         Thence, when they had sprinkled the waters tasted on their vests, and head, they bend their footsteps to the temples of the sacred Goddess; inde ubi libatos inroravere liquores
        vestibus et capiti, flectunt vestigia sanctae
        ad delubra deae,         the tops of which were defiled with foul moss, and the altars were standing without fires. quorum fastigia turpi
        pallebant musco stabantque sine ignibus arae.         When they touched the steps of the temple, each prostrate on the ground, and gave kisses to the icy stone. ut templi tetigere gradus, procumbit uterque
        pronus humi gelidoque pavens dedit oscula saxo         And thus they said; “O Themis, if the deities relent overcome by just prayers, if the anger of the gods is turned, say by what are the loss of our race may be reparable, and, most gentle goddess, bring assistance to our sunken fortunes.” atque ita 'si precibus' dixerunt 'numina iustis
        victa remollescunt, si flectitur ira deorum,
        dic, Themi, qua generis damnum reparabile nostri
        arte sit, et mersis fer opem, mitissima, rebus!'         The goddess was moved, and gave the oracle: “Depart from the temple, and veil the head, and loose your girded garments, and cast the bones of your great parent behind your back.” Mota dea est sortemque dedit: 'discedite templo
        et velate caput cinctasque resolvite vestes
        ossaque post tergum magnae iactate parentis!'         They were astounded a long time and Pyrrha first breaks silence with her voice, and refuses to obey the commands of the goddess; and asks with trembling mouth that she will give excuse to her; and fears to offend her mother’s shades, by casting her bones. obstupuere diu: rumpitque silentia voce
        Pyrrha prior iussisque deae parere recusat,
        detque sibi veniam pavido rogat ore pavetque
        laedere iactatis maternas ossibus umbras.         Meanwhile they repeat with themselves the words of the given oracle, obscure withdark mysteries, and revolve them between themselves. interea repetunt caecis obscura latebris
        verba datae sortis secum inter seque volutant.         After the son of Prometheus soothes the daughter of Epimetheus with gentle words, and says “Aiii either there is some deceitful subtlety against us, or the oracles are pious, and advise no unrighteousness. inde Promethides placidis Epimethida dictis
        mulcet et 'aut fallax' ait 'est sollertia nobis,
        aut (pia sunt nullumque nefas oracula suadent!)         “The earth our great parent; I suppose that the stones in the body of the earth are called bones; we are ordered to cast these behind our back.” magna parens terra est: lapides in corpore terrae
        ossa reor dici; iacere hos post terga iubemur.'         Although the daughter of Titan was moved by the interpretation of her husband, nevertheless hope is in in doubt. Both so distrust the heavenly instructions. But what harm will it do to try? Coniugis augurio quamquam Titania mota est,
        spes tamen in dubio est: adeo caelestibus ambo
        diffidunt monitis; sed quid temptare nocebit?         They descend and cover the head, and ungird their tunics; and throw the stones as commanded behind their footsteps. descendunt: velantque caput tunicasque recingunt
        et iussos lapides sua post vestigia mittunt.         ponere duritiem coepere suumque rigorem

        contigit, ut quaedam, sic non manifesta videri

        non exacta satis rudibusque simillima signis,         Nevertheless the part of them which was moist with any juice, and earthy, was turned to the use of the body; quae tamen ex illis aliquo pars umida suco
        et terrena fuit, versa est in corporis usum;         that which is solid, and cannot be bent, is changed into bones; quod solidum est flectique nequit, mutatur in ossa, That which just now was vein, remained under the same name. quae modo vena fuit, sub eodem nomine mansit, And in a brief space, by the gift of the Gods above, 'the stones thrown by the hands of the man derived a manly appearance, and woman was created from the woman's casting, inque brevi spatio superorum numine saxa
        missa viri manibus faciem traxere virorum

          The Old Kingdom of Egypt
            Apollo did wish for Egypt to prosper And so He gave the remains of the Python to the people of Egypt as the River Nile For five hundred years the people of Egypt owed their lifes to the River Nile Many men fought for control of the River It was Menes, Son of Sol, to unite all of Egypt And so was formed the Old Kingdom of Egypt

          Middle Kingdom of Egypt
            The Pharaohs of Egypt ruled with iron fists They constructed grand buildings to honor only themselves Only the Pharaoh benefited from the labor of the people of Egypt The Gods led the noblemen to revolt Egypt fell into anarchy Noblemen had to cultivate land to survive Relative turned against relative To resolve the fighting, Sol gave half of Egypt to each His two sons Order was restored within their separate realms Each half grew jealous of the other War between the Upper and Lower Egypts erupted in time Upper Egypt conquered Lower Egypt Peace was restored to all of Egypt Pharaohs again had control People demanded better care from the Pharaohs And so was formed the Middle Kingdom of Egypt
          Birth of 𒀭Jupiter
            In the chamber of the Destinies, the room of the archetypes, The wisest of the wise, the sage of the Gods, 𒀭Jupiter was conceived. In Abzu was 𒀭Jupiter born, In pure Abzu was 𒀭Jupiter born. Ea/Enki his Father begat Him, Damkina his Mother bore Him. He sucked the breasts of Goddesses, A nurse reared Him and filled Him with terror. His figure was well developed, the glance of His eyes was dazzling, His growth was manly, He was mighty from the beginning. Caelus, His Father's begetter, saw Him, He exulted and smiled; His heart filled with joy. Caelus rendered Him perfect: His divinity was remarkable, And He became very lofty, excelling Them in His attributes. His members were incomprehensibly wonderful, Incapable of being grasped with the mind, hard even to look on. Four were His eyes, four His ears, Flame shot forth as He moved His lips. His four ears grew large, And His eyes likewise took in everything. His figure was lofty and superior in comparison with the Gods, His limbs were surpassing, His nature was superior. “Mari-utu, Mari-utu, “The Son, the Sun-God, the Sun-God of the Gods.” He was clothed with the aura of the Ten Gods, so exalted was His strength, The Fifty Dreads were loaded upon him.

            FRAGMENT 3 - AEGAEON
Scholiast on Apollonius Rhodius, Arg. i. 1165:
Eumelus says that Aegaeon was the son of Earth and Sea and, having his dwelling in the sea, was an ally of the Titans.

            FRAGMENT 6 - SATURN AND PHILYRA
Scholiast on Apollonius Rhodius, Arg. i. 554:
The author of the War of the Giants says that Saturn took the shape of a horse and lay with Philyra, the daughter of Ocean. Through this cause Chiron was born a centaur: his wife was Chariclo.

            FRAGMENT 7 - HERCULES
Athenaeus, xi. 470 B:
Theolytus says that he (Hercules) sailed across the sea in a cauldron; but the first to give this story is the author of the War of the Titans.
          Fragments of Titanomachy, translated by H. G. Evelyn-White

An hundred arms sprang from the shoulders of all alike, and each had fifty heads growing upon His shoulders upon stout limbs. These, then, stood against the Titans in grim strife, holding huge rocks in their strong hands. And on the other part the Titans eagerly strengthened Their ranks, and both sides at one time showed the work of Their hands and Their might. The boundless sea rang terribly around, and the earth crashed loudly: wide Heaven was shaken and groaned, and high Olympus reeled from its foundation under the charge of the undying Gods, and a heavy quaking reached dim Tartarus and the deep sound of Their feet in the fearful onset and of Their hard missiles. Then 𒀭Jupiter no longer held back His might; but straight His heart was filled with fury and He showed forth all His strength. From Heaven and from Olympus He came forthwith, hurling His lightning: the bold flew thick and fast from His strong hand together with thunder and lightning, whirling an awesome flame. The life-giving earth crashed around in burning, and the vast wood crackled loud with fire all about. All the land seethed, and Ocean's streams and the unfruitful sea. The hot vapour lapped round the earthborn Titans: flame unspeakable rose to the bright upper air: the flashing glare of the thunder-stone and lightning blinded Their eyes for all that there were strong. Astounding heat seized Chaos: and to see with eyes and to hear the sound with ears it seemed even as if Earth and wide Heaven above came together; for such a mighty crash would have arisen if Earth were being hurled to ruin, and Heaven from on high were hurling her down; so great a crash was there while the Gods were meeting together in strife. Also the winds brought rumbling earthquake and duststorm, thunder and lightning and the lurid thunderbolt, which are the shafts of great 𒀭Jupiter, and carried the clangour and the warcry into the midst of the two hosts. A horrible uproar of terrible strife arose: mighty deeds were shown and the battle inclined. But until then, They kept at one another and fought continually in cruel war. For whoever of the deathless Gods that hold the peaks of snowy Olympus pours a libation of Her water is forsworn, lies breathless until a full year is completed, and never comes near to taste ambrosia and nectar, but lies spiritless and voiceless on a strewn bed: and a heavy trance overshadows Him. But when He has spent a long year in His sickness, another penance and a harder follows after the first. For nine years He is cut off from the eternal Gods and never joins Their councils of Their feasts, nine full years. But in the tenth year He comes again to join the assemblies of the deathless Gods who live in the house of Olympus. And there are shining gates and an immoveable threshold of bronze having unending roots and it is grown of itself (25). And beyond, away from all the Gods, live the Titans, beyond gloomy Chaos.             καὶ also πόδες ἀκάματοι κρατεροῦ θεοῦ genitive god·             From his shoulders grew an hundred heads of a snake, a fearful dragon, with dark, flickering tongues, and from under the brows of his eyes in his marvellous heads flashed fire, and fire burned from his heads as he glared. And there were voices in all his dreadful heads which uttered every kind of sound unspeakable; for at one time they made sounds such that the gods understood, but at another, the noise of a bull bellowing aloud in proud ungovernable fury; and at another, the sound of a lion, relentless of heart; and at anothers, sounds like whelps, wonderful to hear; and again, at another, he would hiss, so that the high mountains re-echoed. And truly a thing past help would have happened on that day, and he would have come to reign over mortals and immortals, had not the father of men and gods been quick to perceive it. But he thundered hard and mightily: and the earth around resounded terribly and the wide heaven above, and the sea and Ocean's streams and the nether parts of the earth. Great Olympus reeled beneath the divine feet of the king as he arose and earth groaned thereat. And through the two of them heat took hold on the dark-blue sea, through the thunder and lightning, and through the fire from the monster, and the scorching winds and blazing thunderbolt. The whole earth seethed, and sky and sea: and the long waves raged along the beaches round and about, at the rush of the deathless gods: and there arose an endless shaking. Hades trembled where he rules over the dead below, and the Titans under Tartarus who live with Saturn, because of the unending clamour and the fearful strife. So when 𒀭Jupiter had raised up His might and seized his arms, thunder and lightning and lurid thunderbolt, he leaped from Olympus and struck him, and burned all the marvellous heads of the monster about Him. But when 𒀭Jupiter had conquered him and lashed him with strokes, Typhoeus was hurled down, a maimed wreck, so that the huge earth groaned. And flame shot forth from the thunder-stricken lord in the dim rugged glens of the mount (26), when he was smitten. A great part of huge earth was scorched by the terrible vapour and melted as tin melts when heated by men's art in channelled (27) crucibles; or as iron, which is hardest of all things, is softened by glowing fire in mountain glens and melts in the divine earth through the strength of Vulcan (28). Even so, then, the earth melted in the glow of the blazing fire. And in the bitterness of his anger 𒀭Jupiter cast him into wide Tartarus. ἐκ δέ postpositional but, and οἱ ὤμων genitive shoulders

And from Typhoeus come boisterous winds which blow damply, except Auster and Boreas and clear Zephyr. These are a god-sent kind, and a great blessing to men; but the others blow fitfully upon the seas. Some rush upon the misty sea and work great havoc among men with their evil, raging blasts; for varying with the season they blow, scattering ships and destroying sailors. And men who meet these upon the sea have no help against the mischief. Others again over the boundless, flowering earth spoil the fair fields of men who dwell below, filling them with dust and cruel uproar. But when She was about to bring forth the Goddess bright-eyed Minerva, 𒀭Jupiter craftily deceived Her with cunning words and put Her in his own belly, as Earth and starry Heaven advised. For They advised Him so, to the end that no other should hold royal sway over the eternal Gods in place of 𒀭Jupiter; for very wise children were destined to be born of Her, first the maiden bright-eyed Tritogenia, equal to Her Father in strength and in wise understanding; but afterwards She was to bear a son of overbearing spirit, king of Gods and men. But 𒀭Jupiter put Her into His own belly first, that the Goddess might devise for Him both good and evil.             Circe
            A sorceress in Homer's Odyssey
            And Circe, the daughter of Sol, Hyperion's Son, loved steadfast Odysseus and bare Agrius and Latinus who was faultless and strong; Κίρκη Circe δ᾽ postpositional but Ἠελίου genitive Sol θυγάτηρ daughter Ὑπεριονίδαο Son of Hyperion,
            γείνατ᾽ bore, begot Ὀδυσσῆος genitive Odysseus ταλασίφρονος steadfast of heart ἐν among φιλότητι intercourse

          Caelus Deceives the Canaanites
            The Hebrews were led by Abraham Caelus threatened Abraham and had the Hebrews worship no other Gods but Himself The Sons of Abraham did take heed of His words The Sons of Asshur lived now in Mesopotamia These Assyrians continued to worship the Gods from the tradition of the Sumerians There was much fighting between the Hebrews and the Assyrians Caelus promised the Hebrews more fertile land to the west, in the land of Canaan Abraham instead found the land to be under a drought Abraham agreed with Caelus to have all sons of him to be circumcised Caelus praised Abraham and made many promises to him
          Hammurabi and the First Babylonian Dynasty
            The Amorites worshipped Hadad (𒀭Jupiter) in the land of Canaan They moved into the central plains and founded the city of Babylon (First Babylonian Dynasty, 1894 BCE – 1595 BCE) (Hammurabi, 1792 BCE – 1750 BCE) Hammurabi freed Mesopotamia from Elamite rule Hammurabi removed the power of Enlil in Nippur and proclaimed Marduk as the Supreme God (Sack of Babylon by Hittites, 1595 BCE)
          Dialogue between a Man and His God
          New Kingdom of Egypt
            Egypt was full of power and wealth during this reign People flocked to the grand cities along the Nile Joseph, grandson of Isaac, foretold the coming of famine And famine did spread throughout Egypt Pharaoh Amenemhat invited the Sons of Jacob, son of Isaac, into Egypt Amenemhat lost control of Egypt as the famines continued Pharaoh Neferhotep allowed Egypt to splinter The Hyksos, Sons of Joseph gained control of each new vassal The Hyksos took control of Itj-tawy, honorary capital of Egypt The great city of Thebes in Lower Egypt declared itself the new proper capital of Egypt The leaders of Thebes slowly conquered the Hyksos Pharaoh Amose reclaimed all of Egypt under the rule of Thebes And so was formed the New Kingdom of Egypt
          Minoan Civilization
            In the sea of Neptune slept the island of Crete The Cretans of Crete were born from Pandora, wife of Epimetheus, Son of Iapetus They were led by the stern rule of Minos, Son of Pandora (Height of the Minoan Civilization) Minos begot Lycastus who begot Minos II (Discussion of the Minotaur) Minos II begot Deucalion who begot Idomeneus

          Phoenicians
            From the Amorites came the Phoenicians, who spread themselves across the Mediterranean Sea
